           </i>
           (alongside
           <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_pound" title="Falkland Islands pound">
            Falkland Islands pound
           </a>
           )
          </li>
          <li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0">
           <i>
            <span class="flagicon">
             <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="600" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Flag_of_the_British_Indian_Ocean_Territory.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_British_Indian_Ocean_Territory.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Flag_of_the_British_Indian_Ocean_Territory.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_British_Indian_Ocean_Territory.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Flag_of_the_British_Indian_Ocean_Territory.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_British_Indian_Ocean_Territory.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Ocean_Territory" title="British Indian Ocean Territory">
             British Indian Ocean Territory
            </a>
           </i>
           (
           <i>
            de jure
           </i>
           ,
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._dollar" title="U.S. dollar">
            U.S. dollar
           </a>
           used
           <i>
            de facto
           </i>
           )
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
            <a href="#cite_note-2">
             [2]
            </a>
           </sup>
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">

            </a>
           </sup>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
        <p>

     <p>
      The
      <b>
       pound sterling
      </b>
      (symbol:
      <b>
       <a href="/wiki/Pound_sign" title="Pound sign">
        £
       </a>
      </b>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">
       ISO code
      </a>
      :
      <b>
       GBP
      </b>
      [Great Britain Pound]), commonly known as the
      <b>
       pound
      </b>
      , is the official
      <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">
       currency
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
       United Kingdom
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">
       Jersey
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">
       Guernsey
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
       Isle of Man
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/South_Georgia_and_the_South_Sandwich_Islands" title="South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands">
       South Georgia and the South Sandwich Islands
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/British_Antarctic_Territory" title="British Antarctic Territory">
       British Antarctic Territory
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tristan_da_Cunha" title="Tristan da Cunha">
       Tristan da Cunha
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is subdivided into 100
      <i>
       pence
      </i>
      (singular:
      <i>
       penny
      </i>
      , abbreviated:
      <i>
       p
      </i>
      ). A number of nations that do not use sterling also have currencies called the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pound_(currency)" title="Pound (currency)">
        pound
       </a>
      </i>
      . At various times, the pound sterling was
      <a href="/wiki/Commodity_money" title="Commodity money">
       commodity money
      </a>
      or bank notes backed by silver or gold, but it is currently
      <a href="/wiki/Fiat_money" title="Fiat money">
       fiat money
      </a>
      , backed only by the economy in the areas where it is accepted. The pound sterling is the world's oldest currency still in use and which has been in continuous use since its inception.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rendall_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rendall-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The British
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">
       Crown dependencies
      </a>
      of Guernsey and Jersey produce their own local issues of sterling: the "
      <a href="/wiki/Guernsey_pound" title="Guernsey pound">
       Guernsey pound
      </a>
      " and the "
      <a href="/wiki/Jersey_pound" title="Jersey pound">
       Jersey pound
      </a>
      ". The pound sterling is also used in the Isle of Man (alongside the
      <a href="/wiki/Manx_pound" title="Manx pound">
       Manx pound
      </a>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">
       Gibraltar
      </a>
      (alongside the
      <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar_pound" title="Gibraltar pound">
       Gibraltar pound
      </a>
      ), the
      <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">
       Falkland Islands
      </a>
      (alongside the
      <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_pound" title="Falkland Islands pound">
       Falkland Islands pound
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena" title="Saint Helena">
       Saint Helena
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ascension_Island" title="Ascension Island">
       Ascension Island
      </a>
      in Saint Helena, Ascension and Tristan da Cunha (alongside the
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena_pound" title="Saint Helena pound">
       Saint Helena pound
      </a>
      ). The
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      is the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">
       central bank
      </a>
      for the pound sterling, issuing its own coins and banknotes, and regulating issuance of banknotes by private banks in Scotland and Northern Ireland. Banknotes issued by other jurisdictions are not regulated by the Bank of England; local governments use Bank of England notes as backing for local issuance by allowing them to be exchanged 1:1 at face value.
     </p>
     <p>
      Sterling is the fourth most-traded currency in the
      <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">
       foreign exchange market
      </a>
      , after the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
       United States dollar
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">
       euro
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_yen" title="Japanese yen">
       Japanese yen
      </a>
      . Together with those three currencies it forms the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basket_of_currencies" title="Basket of currencies">
       basket of currencies
      </a>
      which
      <a href="/wiki/Special_drawing_rights#Value_definition" title="Special drawing rights">
       calculate the value
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">
       IMF
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Special_drawing_rights" title="Special drawing rights">
       special drawing rights
      </a>
      , with an 11.3% weighting as of 2011
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (USD 41.9%, Euro 37.4%, Yen 9.4%). Sterling is also the third most-held
      <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">
       reserve currency
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_reserves" title="Foreign exchange reserves">
       global reserves
      </a>
      (about 4%).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers">
         <span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (July 2016)">
          needs update
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>

        <b>
         Obverse
        </b>
        : Portrait of
        <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">
         Elizabeth II
        </a>
        and year of minting.
       </td>
       <td style="width:127.5px;padding:2px 1px 2px 2px; border: 1px #cccccc solid; line-height: 1.5em;">
        <b>
         Reverse
        </b>
        :
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_thistle" title="Scottish thistle">
         Scottish thistle
        </a>
        encircled by the Royal Diadem.
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td colspan="2" style="width:259px;font-size: 85%; padding:2px; line-height: 1.5em;">
        On Obverse,
        <i>
         D.G.REG.F.D
        </i>
        is inscribed (Latin:
        <i>
         ELIZABETH II DEI GRATIA REGINA FIDEI DEFENSATRIX
        </i>
        . English:
        <i>
         Elizabeth II, by the grace of God, Queen and Defender of the Faith.
        </i>
        ). 146,363,501 coins minted in 1984.
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
       Names
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The full, official name, pound sterling, (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_plural#Plurals_of_compound_nouns" title="English plural">
       plural
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       pounds sterling
      </i>
      ) is used mainly in formal contexts and also when it is necessary to distinguish the United Kingdom currency from
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_(currency)" title="Pound (currency)">
       other currencies with the same name
      </a>
      . Otherwise the term
      <i>
       pound
      </i>
      is normally used. The currency name is sometimes abbreviated to just
      <i>
       sterling
      </i>
      , particularly in the wholesale financial markets, but not when referring to specific amounts; for example, "Payment is accepted in sterling" but never "These cost five sterling". The abbreviations "ster." or "stg." are sometimes used. The term "British pound" is commonly used in less formal contexts, although it is not an official name of the currency. The pound sterling is also referred to as "cable" amongst
      <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">
       forex
      </a>
      traders. The origins of this term are attributed to the fact that in the 1800s, the dollar/pound sterling exchange rate was transmitted via transatlantic cable. Forex brokers are sometimes referred to as "cable dealers".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There is apparent convergence of opinion
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="Counter to the variety of citations (September 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      regarding the origin of the term "pound sterling", toward its derivation from the name of a small Norman silver coin,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Entry_189985_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Entry_189985-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and away from its association with Easterlings (Germanic traders) or other etymologies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hence, the Oxford English Dictionary (and sources derived therefrom)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      state that the "most plausible" etymology is derivation from the Old English
      <i>
       steorra
      </i>
      for "star" with the added
      <a href="/wiki/Diminutive" title="Diminutive">
       diminutive
      </a>
      suffix "-ling", to mean "little star" and to refer to a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_penny" title="Silver penny">
       silver penny
      </a>
      of the English Normans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Entry_189985_11-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Entry_189985-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As another established source notes,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica1_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-britannica1-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the compound expression was then derived:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       silver coins known as "sterlings" were issued in the Saxon kingdoms, 240 of them being minted from a pound of silver... Hence, large payments came to be reckoned in "pounds of sterlings," a phrase later shortened...
      </p>
      <div class="templatequotecite">
       <cite>
        —
        <i>
         Encyclopædia Britannica
        </i>
        , entry "pound sterling"
       </cite>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      However, the perceived narrow window of the issuance of this coin, and the fact that coin designs changed frequently in the period in question, led
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_Grierson" title="Philip Grierson">
       Philip Grierson
      </a>
      to reject this in favour of a more complex theory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anglo_saxon_Coins_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Anglo_saxon_Coins-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another argument that the
      <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">
       Hanseatic League
      </a>
      was the origin for both the origin of its definition and manufacture, and in its name is that the German name for the Baltic is "Ost See", or "East Sea", and from this the Baltic merchants were called "Osterlings", or "Easterlings". In 1260, Henry III granted them a charter of protection. And because the League's money was not frequently debased like that of England, English traders stipulated to be paid in pounds of the "Easterlings", which was contracted to "'sterling".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and land for their Kontor, the Steelyard of London, which by the 1340s was also called "Easterlings Hall", or Esterlingeshalle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      For further discussion of the etymology of "sterling", see
      <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">
       sterling silver
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Currency_sign" title="Currency sign">
       currency sign
      </a>
      for the
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_(currency)" title="Pound (currency)">
       pound sign
      </a>
      is
      <b>
       £
      </b>
      , which is usually written with a single cross-bar (as on sterling bank notes), though a version with a double cross-bar (
      <b>
       ₤
      </b>
      ) is also sometimes seen. This symbol derives from medieval Latin documents; the Roman words
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_units_of_measurement" title="Ancient Roman units of measurement">
        libra
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       solidus
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       denarius
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="£sd">
       £sd
      </a>
      ) referred to pounds, shillings and pence
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica1_16-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-britannica1-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      in the British pre-decimal (
      <a href="/wiki/Duodecimal" title="Duodecimal">
       duodecimal
      </a>
      ) currency system and the
      <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">
       black-letter
      </a>
      "L" was the abbreviation for
      <i>
       libra
      </i>
      , the basic Roman unit of weight.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">
       ISO 4217 currency code
      </a>
      is
      <b>
       GBP
      </b>
      . Occasionally, the abbreviation "UKP" is used but this is non-standard because the
      <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166" title="ISO 3166">
       ISO 3166
      </a>
      country code for United Kingdom is GB (see
      <a href="/wiki/Terminology_of_the_British_Isles#Terminology_in_detail" title="Terminology of the British Isles">
       Terminology of the British Isles
      </a>
      ). The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_dependency" title="Crown dependency">
       Crown dependencies
      </a>
      use their own (non-ISO) codes: GGP (
      <a href="/wiki/Guernsey_pound" title="Guernsey pound">
       Guernsey pound
      </a>
      ), JEP (
      <a href="/wiki/Jersey_pound" title="Jersey pound">
       Jersey pound
      </a>
      ) and IMP (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man_pound" title="Isle of Man pound">
       Isle of Man pound
      </a>
      ). Stocks are often traded in pence, so traders may refer to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pence_sterling" title="Pence sterling">
       pence sterling
      </a>
      , GBX (sometimes GBp), when listing stock prices.
     </p>
     <p>
      A common slang term for the pound sterling or pound is
      <i>
       quid
      </i>
      , which is singular and plural, except in the common phrase "Quids in!" The term may have come via Italian immigrants from "
      <a href="/wiki/Italian_scudo" title="Italian scudo">
       scudo
      </a>
      ", the name for a number of coins used in Italy until the 19th century; or from Latin 'quid' via the common phrase
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Quid_pro_quo" title="Quid pro quo">
        quid pro quo
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      literally, "what for what," or, figuratively, "An equal exchange or substitution".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Subdivisions_and_other_units">
       Subdivisions and other units
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Subdivisions and other units">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Decimal_coinage">
       Decimal coinage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Decimal coinage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Since
      <a href="/wiki/Decimalisation" title="Decimalisation">
       decimalisation
      </a>
      in 1971 (see
      <a href="/wiki/Decimal_Day" title="Decimal Day">
       Decimal Day
      </a>
      ), the pound has been divided into 100 pence (until 1981 described on the coinage as "new pence"). The symbol for the penny is "p"; hence an amount such as 50p (£0.50) properly pronounced "fifty pence" is more colloquially, quite often, pronounced "fifty pee" /fɪfti: pi:/. This also helped to distinguish between new and old pence amounts during the changeover to the decimal system. A decimal
      <a href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British decimal coin)">
       halfpenny
      </a>
      was issued until 1984 in an attempt to stop inflation but was removed due to having a higher cost to manufacture than its face value.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-decimal">
       Pre-decimal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pre-decimal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/%C2%A3sd" title="£sd">
       £sd
      </a>
     </div>

        <div class="magnify">
