         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Hatter" title="The Hatter">
         The Hatter
        </a>
        's hat shows an example of the old pre-decimal system: the hat costs half a guinea (10 shillings and 6 pence).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Prior to decimalisation, the pound was divided into 20
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_shilling_coin" title="British shilling coin">
       shillings
      </a>
      and each shilling into 12
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_penny_sterling" title="Old penny sterling">
       pence
      </a>
      , making 240 pence to the pound. The symbol for the shilling was "
      <i>
       s
      </i>
      ."—not from the first letter of the word, but from the Latin
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">
        solidus
       </a>
      </i>
      . The symbol for the penny was "
      <i>
       d
      </i>
      .", from the French
      <i>
       denier
      </i>
      , from the Latin
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">
        denarius
       </a>
      </i>
      (the solidus and denarius were Roman coins). A mixed sum of shillings and pence, such as 3 shillings and 6 pence, was written as "3/6" or "3
      <i>
       s
      </i>
      . 6
      <i>
       d
      </i>
      ." and spoken as "three and six" (except for "1/1," "2/1" etc., which were spoken as "one and a penny," "two and a penny," etc.). 5 shillings was written as "5
      <i>
       s
      </i>
      ." or, more commonly, "5/–". The stroke (/) indicating shillings is also known as a solidus and was originally an adaptation of the
      <a href="/wiki/Long_s" title="Long s">
       long s
      </a>
      which represented that word.
     </p>
     <p>
      Various coin denominations had, and in some cases continue to have, special names—such as
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">
       crown
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">
       farthing
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">
       sovereign
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" title="Guinea (British coin)">
       guinea
      </a>
      . See
      <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">
       Coins of the pound sterling
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_British_bank_notes_and_coins" title="List of British bank notes and coins">
       List of British coins and banknotes
      </a>
      for details.
     </p>
     <p>
      By the 1950s, coins of Kings
      <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">
       George III
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">
       George IV
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">
       William IV
      </a>
      had disappeared from circulation, but coins (at least the penny) bearing the head of any British king or queen from
      <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">
       Queen Victoria
      </a>
      onwards could be found in circulation. Silver coins were replaced by those in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cupro-nickel" title="Cupro-nickel">
       cupro-nickel
      </a>
      in 1947 and by the 1960s the silver coins were rarely seen. Silver/cupro-nickel shillings (from any period after 1816) and
      <a href="/wiki/Two_shilling_coin" title="Two shilling coin">
       florins
      </a>
      (2 shillings) remained as legal tender after decimalisation (as 5p and 10p respectively) until 1993 and are now officially demonetised.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The pound sterling is the world's oldest currency still in use.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rendall_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rendall-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anglo-Saxon">
       Anglo-Saxon
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Anglo-Saxon">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_pound" title="Anglo-Saxon pound">
       Anglo-Saxon pound
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Pound.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="908" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/The_Pound.jpg/220px-The_Pound.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/The_Pound.jpg/330px-The_Pound.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/The_Pound.jpg/440px-The_Pound.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Pound.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A pound = 20
        <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">
         shillings
        </a>
        = 240
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silver_penny" title="Silver penny">
         silver pennies
        </a>
        (formerly)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       pound
      </i>
      was a unit of account in
      <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">
       Anglo-Saxon
      </a>
      England, equal to 240 silver
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(c._600-1066)" title="History of the English penny (c. 600-1066)">
       pennies
      </a>
      and equivalent to one
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">
       pound weight
      </a>
      of silver. It evolved into the modern British
      <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">
       currency
      </a>
      , the pound sterling.
     </p>
     <p>
      The accounting system of 4
      <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">
       farthings
      </a>
      = 1
      <a href="/wiki/Penny" title="Penny">
       penny
      </a>
      , 12 pence = 1
      <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">
       shilling
      </a>
      , 20 shillings = 1 pound was adopted from that introduced by
      <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
       Charlemagne
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">
       Frankish Empire
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/French_livre" title="French livre">
       French livre
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      The origins of sterling lie in the reign of King
      <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Mercia" title="Offa of Mercia">
       Offa of Mercia
      </a>
      , (757–796) who introduced the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(c._600-1066)" title="History of the English penny (c. 600-1066)">
       silver penny
      </a>
      . It copied the
      <i>
       denarius
      </i>
      of the new currency system of
      <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
       Charlemagne
      </a>
      's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">
       Frankish Empire
      </a>
      . As in the Carolingian system, 240 pennies weighed 1
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">
       pound
      </a>
      (corresponding to Charlemagne's
      <i>
       libra
      </i>
      ), with the shilling corresponding to Charlemagne's
      <i>
       solidus
      </i>
      and equal to 12d. At the time of the penny's introduction, it weighed 22.5 troy
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_(mass)" title="Grain (mass)">
       grains
      </a>
      of fine silver (32 tower grains; about 1.5 g), indicating that the Mercian pound weighed 5,400 troy grains (the Mercian pound became the basis of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tower_pound" title="Tower pound">
       tower pound
      </a>
      , which weighed 5,400 troy grains, equivalent to 7,680 tower grains, about 350g). At this time, the name sterling had yet to be acquired. The penny swiftly spread throughout the other Anglo-Saxon kingdoms and became the standard coin of what was to become England.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="is some of this from Adam smith? (September 2016)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">
       Medieval
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Medieval">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The early pennies were struck from fine silver (as pure as was available). However,
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(1154%E2%80%931485)" title="History of the English penny (1154–1485)">
       in 1158, a new coinage
      </a>
      was introduced by King
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">
       Henry II
      </a>
      (known as the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(1154%E2%80%931485)" title="History of the English penny (1154–1485)">
        Tealby penny
       </a>
      </i>
      ) which was struck from 0.925 (92.5%) silver. This became the standard until the 20th century and is today known as
      <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">
       sterling silver
      </a>
      , named after its association with the currency. Sterling silver is harder than the 0.999 (99.9%)
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fine_silver" title="Fine silver">
       fine silver
      </a>
      that was traditionally used and so sterling silver coins did not wear down as rapidly as fine silver coins. The English currency was almost exclusively silver until 1344, when the gold
      <a href="/wiki/Noble_(English_coin)" title="Noble (English coin)">
       noble
      </a>
      was successfully introduced into circulation. However, silver remained the legal basis for sterling until 1816.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the time of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">
       Henry III
      </a>
      , the pound Sterling equaled the pound weight Tower.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ViewOfCoinage_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ViewOfCoinage-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 28th year of Edward I, the Tale Pound, or Pound Sterling, first began to differ or come short of the Pound weight Tower, from which it drew its origine; and to which until now it was equal, for by indenture of that year the pound weight was to contain twenty shillings and three-pence in Tale.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ViewOfCoinage_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ViewOfCoinage-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :14
      </sup>
      In the 27th year of Edward III, the pound Sterling was now but 4/5 of the pound weight, or 9 oz, 12 dwts. Tower.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ViewOfCoinage_22-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ViewOfCoinage-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :15
      </sup>
      By an act of 13 Henry IV. the pound weight of Standard silver was to contain thirty shillings in Tale, or 1-1/2 pound Sterling, therefore the pound Sterling reduced to 2/3 of a pound weight, or 8 oz. Tower.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ViewOfCoinage_22-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-ViewOfCoinage-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :18
      </sup>
      The pound Sterling was adjusted in weight several more times thereafter.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">
       Henry IV
      </a>
      (1399–1413), the penny was reduced in weight to 15 grains (0.97 g) of silver, with a further reduction to 12 grains (0.78 g) in 1464.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tudor">
       Tudor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tudor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      During the reigns of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">
       Henry VIII
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI" title="Edward VI">
       Edward VI
      </a>
      , the silver coinage was drastically debased, although the pound was redefined to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troy_pound" title="Troy pound">
       troy pound
      </a>
      of 5,760 grains (373 g) in 1526. In 1544, a silver coinage was issued containing just one third silver and two thirds copper—equating to .333 silver, or 33.3% pure. The result was a coin copper in appearance, but relatively pale in colour. In 1552, a new silver coinage was introduced, struck in
      <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">
       sterling silver
      </a>
      . However, the penny's weight was reduced to 8 grains (0.52 g), meaning that 1 troy pound of sterling silver produced 60 shillings of coins. This silver standard was known as the "60-shilling standard" and lasted until 1601 when a "62-shilling standard" was introduced, reducing the penny's weight to
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       7
       <span class="visualhide">
       </span>
       <sup>
        23
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        31
       </sub>
      </span>
      grains (0.50 g).
     </p>
     <p>
      Throughout this period, the size and value of the gold coinage fluctuated considerably.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Unofficial_gold_standard">
       Unofficial gold standard
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Unofficial gold standard">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1663, a new
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gold_coinage" title="Gold coinage">
       gold coinage
      </a>
      was introduced based on the 22
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carat_(purity)" title="Carat (purity)">
       carat
      </a>
      fine
      <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(coin)" title="Guinea (coin)">
       guinea
      </a>
      . Fixed in weight at
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       44
       <span class="visualhide">
       </span>
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
      to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troy_pound" title="Troy pound">
       troy pound
      </a>
      from 1670, this coin's value varied considerably until 1717, when it was fixed at 21 shillings (21/-, 1.05 pounds).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, despite the efforts of Sir
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">
       Isaac Newton
      </a>
      , Master of the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">
       Mint
      </a>
      , to reduce the guinea's value, this valuation overvalued gold relative to silver when compared to the valuations in other European countries.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gresham%27s_Law" title="Gresham's Law">
       British merchants sent silver abroad in payments whilst goods for export were paid for with gold. As a consequence
      </a>
      , silver flowed out of the country and gold flowed in, leading to a situation where Great Britain was effectively on a
      <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">
       gold standard
      </a>
      . Aggravating this outflow was the fact that silver was the only commodity accepted by China for exporting goods during this period. From the mid-17th century, around 28 million kilograms (62 million pounds) of silver were received by China, principally from European powers, in exchange for Chinese tea and other goods. In order to trade with China, Great Britain had to first trade with the other European nations to receive silver, which led to the
      <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">
       East India Company
      </a>
      redressing this trade imbalance through the
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_opium_in_China" title="History of opium in China">
       indirect sale
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">
       opium
      </a>
      to the Chinese.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Domestic offtake further reduced silver in circulation as the improving fortunes of the merchant class led to increased demand for tablewares. Silversmiths had always regarded coinage as a source of raw material, already government verified for fineness. As a result, sterling coins were being melted and fashioned into sterling silverware at an accelerating rate. A 1697 Act of Parliament attempted to stem this tide by raising the minimum acceptable fineness on wrought plate from sterling's 92.5% to a new
      <a href="/wiki/Britannia_silver" title="Britannia silver">
       Britannia silver
      </a>
      standard of 95.83%. Product then made solely from melted coins would be found wanting when the silversmith took his wares to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assay_Office" title="Assay Office">
       Assay Office
      </a>
      , thus discouraging the melting of coins.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_modern_currency">
       Establishment of modern currency
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Establishment of modern currency">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      was founded in 1694, followed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">
       Bank of Scotland
      </a>
      a year later. Both began to issue
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_money" title="Paper money">
       paper money
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Currency_of_Great_Britain_.281707.29_and_the_United_Kingdom_.281801.29">
       Currency of Great Britain (1707) and the United Kingdom (1801)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Currency of Great Britain (1707) and the United Kingdom (1801)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_Scots" title="Pound Scots">
       pound Scots
      </a>
      once had much the same value as the pound sterling, but it suffered far higher devaluation until in the 17th century it was pegged to sterling at a value of 12 pounds Scots = 1 pound sterling.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1707, the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">
       Kingdom of England
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">
       Kingdom of Scotland
      </a>
      merged to form the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">
       Kingdom of Great Britain
      </a>
      . In accordance with the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">
       Treaty of Union
      </a>
      , the currency of Great Britain was sterling, with the pound Scots soon being replaced by sterling at the pegged value.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1801, Great Britain and the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">
       Kingdom of Ireland
      </a>
      were united to form the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">
       United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland
      </a>
      . However, the
      <a href="/wiki/Irish_pound" title="Irish pound">
       Irish pound
      </a>
      continued to exist and was not replaced by sterling until January 1826. The conversion rate had long been thirteen Irish pounds to twelve pounds sterling.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_the_Empire">
       Use in the Empire
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Use in the Empire">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Sterling_area" title="Sterling area">
       Sterling area
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Sterling circulated in much of the
      <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">
       British Empire
      </a>
      . In some parts, it was used alongside local currencies. For example, the gold sovereign was legal tender in Canada despite the use of the
      <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">
       Canadian dollar
      </a>
      . Several colonies and dominions adopted the pound as their own currency. These included Australia,
      <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">
       Barbados
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/British_West_Africa" title="British West Africa">
       British West Africa
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Cyprus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">
       Fiji
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">
       Irish Free State
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">
       Jamaica
      </a>
      , New Zealand, South Africa and
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">
       Southern Rhodesia
      </a>
      . Some of these retained parity with sterling throughout their existence (e.g. the
      <a href="/wiki/South_African_pound" title="South African pound">
       South African pound
      </a>
      ), whilst others deviated from parity after the end of the gold standard (e.g. the
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_pound" title="Australian pound">
       Australian pound
      </a>
      ). These currencies and others tied to sterling constituted the
      <a href="/wiki/Sterling_area" title="Sterling area">
       sterling area
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The original English colonies on mainland North America were not party to the sterling area because the above-mentioned silver shortage in England coincided with these colonies' formative years. As a result of equitable trade (and rather less equitable piracy), the Spanish milled dollar became the most common coin within the English colonies.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Gold_standard">
       Gold standard
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Gold standard">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">
       American war of independence and Napoleonic wars
      </a>
      , Bank of England notes were
      <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">
       legal tender
      </a>
      and their value floated relative to gold. The Bank also issued silver tokens to alleviate the shortage of silver coins. In 1816, the
      <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">
       gold standard
      </a>
      was adopted officially, with the silver standard reduced to 66 shillings (66/-, £3 6s), rendering silver coins a "token" issue (i.e., not containing their value in precious metal). In 1817, the
      <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">
       sovereign
      </a>
      was introduced, valued at 20 shillings. Struck in 22‑carat gold, it contained 113 grains (7.3 g) of gold and replaced the guinea as the standard British gold coin without changing the gold standard. In 1825, the
      <a href="/wiki/Irish_pound" title="Irish pound">
       Irish pound
      </a>
      , which had been pegged to sterling since 1801 at a rate of 13 Irish pounds = 12 pounds sterling, was replaced, at the same rate, with sterling.
     </p>
     <p>
      By the 19th century the pound sterling was widely accepted outside Britain. The American
      <a href="/wiki/Nellie_Bly" title="Nellie Bly">
       Nellie Bly
      </a>
      carried Bank of England notes on
      <a href="/wiki/Around_the_World_in_Seventy-Two_Days" title="Around the World in Seventy-Two Days">
       her 1889-1890 trip around the world in 72 days
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bly1890_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bly1890-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During the late 19th and early 20th centuries, many other countries adopted the gold standard. As a consequence, conversion rates between different currencies could be determined simply from the respective gold standards. The pound sterling was equal to 4.85 United States dollars, 5.25
      <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">
       Canadian dollars
      </a>
      , 12.10
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_guilder" title="Dutch guilder">
       Dutch guilders
      </a>
      , 26.28
      <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">
       French francs
      </a>
      (or equivalent currencies in the
      <a href="/wiki/Latin_Monetary_Union" title="Latin Monetary Union">
       Latin Monetary Union
      </a>
      ), 20.43
      <a href="/wiki/German_gold_mark" title="German gold mark">
       German Marks
      </a>
      or 24.02
      <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_krone" title="Austro-Hungarian krone">
       Austro-Hungarian Krones
      </a>
      . Discussions took place following the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Conferences" title="International Monetary Conferences">
       International Monetary Conference of 1867
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">
       Paris
      </a>
      concerning the possibility of the UK joining the
      <a href="/wiki/Latin_Monetary_Union" title="Latin Monetary Union">
       Latin Monetary Union
      </a>
      , and a
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" title="Royal Commission">
       Royal Commission
      </a>
      on International Coinage examined the issues,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      resulting in a decision against joining monetary union.
     </p>
     <p>
      The gold standard was suspended at the outbreak of the war in 1914, with Bank of England and Treasury notes becoming legal tender. Prior to
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      , the United Kingdom had one of the world's strongest economies, holding 40% of the world's overseas investments. However, after the end of the war the country was indebted. Britain owed £850 million (£37.3 billion as of 2015),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      mostly to the United States, with interest costing the country some 40% of all government spending.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In an attempt to resume stability, a variation on the gold standard was reintroduced in 1925, under which the currency was fixed to gold at its pre-war peg, although people were only able to exchange their currency for gold bullion, rather than for coins. This was abandoned on 21 September 1931, during the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">
       Great Depression
      </a>
      , and sterling suffered an initial devaluation of some 25%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bretton_Woods">
       Bretton Woods
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bretton Woods">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_United_Kingdom#1945.E2.80.931959:_the_post-War_era" title="Economic history of the United Kingdom">
       Economic history of the UK, 1945–59
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1940, an agreement with the U.S.A. pegged the pound to the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
       U.S. dollar
      </a>
      at a rate of £1 = $4.03. (Only the year before, it had been $4.86.)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This rate was maintained through the
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       Second World War
      </a>
      and became part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">
       Bretton Woods system
      </a>
      which governed post-war exchange rates. Under continuing economic pressure, and despite months of denials that it would do so, on 19 September 1949 the government devalued the pound by 30.5% to $2.80. The move prompted several other currencies to be devalued against the dollar.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Bernhard" title="Operation Bernhard">
       Operation Bernhard
      </a>
      was the codename of a secret Nazi plan devised during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">
       Second World War
      </a>
      by the RSHA and the SS to destabilise the British economy via economic warfare by flooding the global economy and the British Empire with forged Bank of England £5, £10, £20, and £50 notes.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1961, 1964 and 1966, the pound came under renewed pressure since the exchange rate against the dollar was considered too high. In the summer of 1966, with the value of the pound falling in the currency markets, exchange controls were tightened by the
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">
       Wilson
      </a>
      government. Among the measures, tourists were banned from taking more than £50 out of the country in travellers' cheques and remittances, plus £15 in cash, until the restriction was lifted in 1979. The pound was devalued by 14.3% to $2.40 on 18 November 1967.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Decimalisation">
       Decimalisation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Decimalisation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Decimal_Day" title="Decimal Day">
       Decimal Day
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Until decimalisation, amounts were stated in pounds, shillings, and pence, with various widely understood notations. The same amount was denoted by 32s 6d, 32/6, £1 12s 6d, £1/12/6. It was customary to specify some prices (for example professional fees and auction prices for works of art) in guineas (one guinea was 21 shillings) although
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" title="Guinea (British coin)">
       guinea coins
      </a>
      were no longer in use.
     </p>
     <p>
      Formal parliamentary proposals to decimalise sterling were first made in 1824 when Sir John Wrottesley, MP for Staffordshire, asked in the British
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons" title="House of Commons">
       House of Commons
      </a>
      whether consideration had been given to decimalising the currency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Wrottesley raised the issue in the House of Commons again in 1833,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it was again raised by
      <a href="/wiki/John_Bowring" title="John Bowring">
       John Bowring
      </a>
      , MP for Kilmarnock Burghs, in 1847
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
      whose efforts led to the introduction in 1848 of what was in effect the first decimal coin in the United Kingdom, the florin, valued at one-tenth of a pound sterling. However, full decimalisation was resisted, although the florin coin, re-designated as
      <i>
       ten new pence
      </i>
      , survived the transfer to a full decimal system in 1971, with examples surviving in British coinage until 1993.
     </p>
     <p>
      J. B. Smith, MP for
      <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Burghs_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Stirling Burghs (UK Parliament constituency)">
       Stirling Burghs
      </a>
      , raised the issue of full decimalisation again in Parliament in 1853,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      resulting in the Chancellor of the Exchequer,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" title="William Gladstone">
       William Gladstone
      </a>
      , announcing soon afterwards that "the great question of a decimal coinage" was "now under serious consideration".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A full proposal for the decimalisation of sterling was then tabled in the House of Commons in June 1855, by William Brown, MP for
      <a href="/wiki/South_Lancashire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South Lancashire (UK Parliament constituency)">
       Lancashire Southern
      </a>
      , with the suggestion that the pound sterling be divided into one thousand parts, each called a "mil", or alternatively a farthing, as the pound was then equivalent to 960 farthings which could easily be rounded up to one thousand farthings in the new system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This did not result in the conversion of the pound sterling into a decimal system, but it was agreed to establish a
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Commission" title="Royal Commission">
       Royal Commission
      </a>
      to look into the issue.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, largely due to the hostility to decimalisation of two of the appointed commissioners,
      <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Jones-Loyd,_1st_Baron_Overstone" title="Samuel Jones-Loyd, 1st Baron Overstone">
       Lord Overstone
      </a>
      (a banker) and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Hubbard,_1st_Baron_Addington" title="John Hubbard, 1st Baron Addington">
       John Hubbard
      </a>
      (Governor of the Bank of England), decimalisation in Britain was effectively quashed for over a hundred years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However the pound sterling was decimalised in various British colonial territories before the United Kingdom, and in several cases using William Brown's proposal that the pound be divided into 1000 parts, called mils. This included Hong Kong from 1863 to 1866;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cyprus from 1955 until 1960 (and continued on the island as the division of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_pound" title="Cypriot pound">
       Cypriot pound
      </a>
      until 1983); and the Palestine Mandate from 1926 until 1948.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Towards the end of the Second World War, various attempts to decimalise the pound sterling in the United Kingdom were made, until in 1966 the British government decided to include in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Speech" title="Queen's Speech">
       Queen's Speech
      </a>
      a plan to convert the pound into a decimal currency.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a result of this, on 15 February 1971, the UK decimalised the pound sterling, replacing the shilling and penny with a single subdivision, the new penny. For example, a price tag of £1 12s 6d became
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       £1.62
       <span class="visualhide">
       </span>
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
      . The word "new" was omitted from coins minted after 1981.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Free-floating_pound">
       Free-floating pound
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Free-floating pound">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      With the breakdown of the
      <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_system" title="Bretton Woods system">
       Bretton Woods system
      </a>
      , the pound
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Floating_currency" title="Floating currency">
       floated
      </a>
      from August 1971 onwards. At first it appreciated a little, rising to almost $2.65 in March 1972 from $2.42, the upper bound of the band in which it had been fixed. The
      <a href="/wiki/Sterling_area" title="Sterling area">
       sterling area
      </a>
      effectively ended at this time when the majority of its members also chose to float freely against the pound and the dollar.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1976_sterling_crisis">
       1976 sterling crisis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 1976 sterling crisis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">
       James Callaghan
      </a>
      became Prime Minister in 1976. He was immediately told the economy was facing huge problems, according to documents released in 2006 by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Archives" title="National Archives">
       National Archives
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The effects of the
      <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">
       1973 oil crisis
      </a>
      were still being felt, with inflation rising to over 27% in 1975.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Financial markets were beginning to believe the pound was overvalued, and in April that year
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">
        The Wall Street Journal
       </a>
      </i>
      advised the sale of sterling investments in the face of high taxes, in a story that ended with "goodbye, Great Britain. It was nice knowing you".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the time the UK government was running a budget deficit, and Labour's strategy emphasised high public spending. Callaghan was told there were three possible outcomes: a disastrous free fall in sterling, an internationally unacceptable siege economy, or a deal with key allies to prop up the pound while painful economic reforms were put in place. The US government feared the crisis could endanger NATO and the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">
       European Economic Community
      </a>
      (EEC), and in light of this the US Treasury set out to force domestic policy changes. In November 1976 the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">
       International Monetary Fund
      </a>
      (IMF) announced the conditions for a loan, including deep cuts in
      <a href="/wiki/Public_expenditure" title="Public expenditure">
       public expenditure
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="1979.E2.80.9389">
       1979–89
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 1979–89">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Conservative Party was elected to office in 1979, on a programme of fiscal austerity. Initially the pound rocketed, moving above US$2.40, as interest rates rose in response to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetarist" title="Monetarist">
       monetarist
      </a>
      policy of targeting
      <a href="/wiki/Money_supply" title="Money supply">
       money supply
      </a>
      . The high exchange rate was widely blamed for the deep
      <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">
       recession
      </a>
      of 1981. Sterling fell sharply after 1980; at its lowest, the pound stood at just $1.03 in March 1985, before rising to $1.70 in December 1989.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samson_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Samson-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Following_the_Deutsche_Mark">
       Following the Deutsche Mark
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Following the Deutsche Mark">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1988,
      <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">
       Margaret Thatcher
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">
       Chancellor of the Exchequer
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">
       Nigel Lawson
      </a>
      , decided that the pound should "shadow" the West German
      <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">
       Deutsche Mark
      </a>
      , with the unintended result of a rapid rise in inflation as the economy boomed due to inappropriately low interest rates. (For ideological reasons, the Conservative Government declined to use alternative mechanisms to control the explosion of credit. For this reason, former Prime Minister
      <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">
       Edward Heath
      </a>
      referred to Lawson as a "one club golfer").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Following
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_re-unification" title="German re-unification">
       German re-unification
      </a>
      in 1990, the reverse held true, as high borrowing costs to fund Eastern reconstruction, a need exacerbated by the political choice to make the Ostmark equivalent to the Deutsche Mark (DM), meant rates in other countries shadowing the DM, especially the UK, were far too high relative to domestic circumstances, leading to a housing decline and recession.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Following_the_European_Currency_Unit">
       Following the European Currency Unit
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Following the European Currency Unit">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On 8 October 1990 the Conservative government (
      <a href="/wiki/Third_Thatcher_ministry" title="Third Thatcher ministry">
       Third Thatcher ministry
      </a>
      ) decided to join the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" title="European Exchange Rate Mechanism">
       European Exchange Rate Mechanism
      </a>
      (ERM), with the pound set at
      <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Mark" title="Deutsche Mark">
       DM
      </a>
      2.95. However, the country was forced to withdraw from the system on "
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">
       Black Wednesday
      </a>
      " (16 September 1992) as Britain's economic performance made the exchange rate unsustainable. Speculator
      <a href="/wiki/George_Soros" title="George Soros">
       George Soros
      </a>
      infamously made approximately US$1 billion from
      <a href="/wiki/Short_(finance)" title="Short (finance)">
       shorting
      </a>
      the pound.
     </p>
     <p>
      'Black Wednesday' saw interest rates jump from 10% to 15% in an unsuccessful attempt to stop the pound from falling below the ERM limits. The exchange rate fell to DM2.20. Those who had argued
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      for a lower GBP/DM exchange rate were vindicated as the cheaper pound encouraged exports and contributed to the economic prosperity of the 1990s.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Following_inflation_targets">
       Following inflation targets
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Following inflation targets">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1997, the newly elected
      <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">
       Labour
      </a>
      government handed over day-to-day control of interest rates to the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      (a policy that had originally been advocated by the
      <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats" title="Liberal Democrats">
       Liberal Democrats
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Bank is now responsible for setting its base rate of interest so as to keep inflation in the
      <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Price_Index_(United_Kingdom)" title="Consumer Price Index (United Kingdom)">
       Consumer Price Index
      </a>
      (CPI) very close to 2%. Should CPI inflation be more than one percentage point above or below the target, the governor of the Bank of England is required to write an open letter to the
      <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">
       Chancellor of the Exchequer
      </a>
      explaining the reasons for this and the measures which will be taken to bring this measure of inflation back in line with the 2% target. On 17 April 2007, CPI inflation was reported at 3.1% (inflation of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retail_Prices_Index_(United_Kingdom)" title="Retail Prices Index (United Kingdom)">
       Retail Prices Index
      </a>
      was 4.8%). Accordingly, and for the first time, the Governor had to write publicly to the government explaining why inflation was more than one percentage point higher than its target.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Euro">
       Euro
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Euro">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_and_the_euro" title="United Kingdom and the euro">
       United Kingdom and the euro
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      As a member of the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
       European Union
      </a>
      , the United Kingdom could have adopted the
      <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">
       euro
      </a>
      as its currency. However, the subject was always politically controversial, and the UK negotiated an opt-out on this issue. The
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">
       2016 referendum
      </a>
      which started the process of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_withdrawal_from_the_European_Union" title="United Kingdom withdrawal from the European Union">
       United Kingdom withdrawal from the European Union
      </a>
      makes adoption of the euro highly unlikely in the near future.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2007,
      <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">
       Gordon Brown
      </a>
      , then
      <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">
       Chancellor of the Exchequer
      </a>
      , ruled out membership for the foreseeable future, saying that the decision not to join had been right for Britain and for Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The government of former Prime Minister
      <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">
       Tony Blair
      </a>
      had pledged to hold a public referendum to decide on membership should "
      <a href="/wiki/Five_economic_tests" title="Five economic tests">
       five economic tests
      </a>
      " be met, to ensure that adoption of the euro would be in the national interest. In addition to these internal (national) criteria, the UK would have to meet the European Union's economic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convergence_criteria" title="Convergence criteria">
       convergence criteria
      </a>
      (Maastricht criteria), before being allowed to adopt the euro. The Conservative and Liberal Democrat coalition government (2010-2015) ruled out joining the euro for that parliamentary term. Currently,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers">
         <span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2016)">
          when?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      the UK's annual
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Government_budget_deficit" title="Government budget deficit">
       government deficit
      </a>
      , as a percentage of the
      <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">
       GDP
      </a>
      , is above the defined threshold.
     </p>
     <p>
      The idea of replacing the pound with the euro was always controversial with the British public, partly because of the pound's identity as a symbol of British sovereignty and because it would, according to many critics, have led to suboptimal interest rates, harming the British economy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In December 2008, the results of a BBC poll of 1000 people suggested that 71% would vote no to the euro, 23% would vote yes to joining the European single currency, while 6% said they were unsure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The pound did not join the Second European Exchange Rate Mechanism (ERM II) after the euro was created.
      <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
       Denmark
      </a>
      and the UK have opt-outs from entry to the euro. Technically, every other EU nation must eventually sign up.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Conservative_Party" title="Scottish Conservative Party">
       Scottish Conservative Party
      </a>
      claimed that there was an issue for Scotland in that the adoption of the euro would mean the end of nationally distinctive banknotes, as the euro banknotes do not have national designs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before the No vote in the
      <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">
       Scottish independence referendum in 2014
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">
       Scottish National Party
      </a>
      affirmed that the Euro would not be the national currency of an independent Scotland.
     </p>
     <p>
      On 1 January 2008, the British sovereign bases on Cyprus (
      <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_and_Dhekelia" title="Akrotiri and Dhekelia">
       Akrotiri and Dhekelia
      </a>
      ) began using the euro (along with the
      <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">
       Republic of Cyprus
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>

       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         NOK (kr)
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.7%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         15
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/23px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/35px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_South_Korea.svg/45px-Flag_of_South_Korea.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>

         South Korean won
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         KRW (₩)
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.6%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">

        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Flag_of_Turkey.svg/23px-Flag_of_Turkey.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Flag_of_Turkey.svg/35px-Flag_of_Turkey.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Flag_of_Turkey.svg/45px-Flag_of_Turkey.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>

        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">

       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.4%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         17
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1350" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_India.svg/23px-Flag_of_India.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_India.svg/35px-Flag_of_India.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/41/Flag_of_India.svg/45px-Flag_of_India.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>

        </a>
       </td>

        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">

        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.1%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         18
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>

       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
