        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.1%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         19
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="720" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/22px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/33px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/43px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 2x" width="22"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_real" title="Brazilian real">
         Brazilian real
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         BRL (R$)
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.0%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         20
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:left;">
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/23px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/35px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Flag_of_South_Africa.svg/45px-Flag_of_South_Africa.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/South_African_rand" title="South African rand">
         South African rand
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        <div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">
         ZAR (R)
        </div>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        1.0%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td colspan="3" style="text-align:right;">
        Other
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        7.1%
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td colspan="3" style="text-align:right;">
        <b>
         Total
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td style="text-align:right;">
        <b>
         200.0%
        </b>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The pound and the euro fluctuate in value against one another, although there may be correlation between movements in their respective exchange rates with other currencies such as the US dollar. Inflation concerns in the UK led the Bank of England to raise interest rates in late 2006 and 2007. This caused the pound to appreciate against other major currencies and, with the US dollar depreciating at the same time, the pound hit a 15-year high against the US dollar on 18 April 2007, reaching US$2 the day before, for the first time since 1992. The pound and many other currencies continued to appreciate against the dollar; sterling hit a 26-year high of US$2.1161 on 7 November 2007 as the dollar fell worldwide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      From mid-2003 to mid-2007, the pound/euro rate remained range-bound (within ± 5%) of €1.45.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_financial_crisis_of_2008%E2%80%932009" title="Global financial crisis of 2008–2009">
       global financial crisis
      </a>
      in late 2008, the pound depreciated sharply, reaching £1 per $1.38 (US) on 23 January 2009
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and falling below €1.25 against the euro in April 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A further decline occurred during the remainder of 2008, most dramatically on 29 December when its euro rate hit an all-time low at €1.0219, while its US dollar rate depreciated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The pound appreciated in early 2009 reaching a peak against the euro in mid-July of €1.17. The following months the pound remained broadly steady against the euro, with the pound's current (27 May 2011) value at €1.15 and US$1.65.
     </p>
     <p>
      On 5 March 2009, the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      announced that it would pump £75 billion of new
      <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">
       capital
      </a>
      into the
      <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Economy of the United Kingdom">
       British economy
      </a>
      , through a process known as
      <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_easing" title="Quantitative easing">
       quantitative easing
      </a>
      . This was the first time in the United Kingdom's history that this measure had been used, although the Bank's
      <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_the_Bank_of_England" title="Governor of the Bank of England">
       Governor
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Mervyn_King,_Baron_King_of_Lothbury" title="Mervyn King, Baron King of Lothbury">
       Mervyn King
      </a>
      suggested it was not an experiment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBC-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The process saw the Bank of England creating new money for itself, which it then used to purchase
      <a href="/wiki/Asset" title="Asset">
       assets
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">
       government bonds
      </a>
      , secured
      <a href="/wiki/Commercial_paper" title="Commercial paper">
       commercial paper
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Corporate_bond" title="Corporate bond">
       corporate bonds
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN1_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CNN1-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The initial amount stated to be created through this method was £75 billion, although
      <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">
       Chancellor of the Exchequer
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Alistair_Darling" title="Alistair Darling">
       Alistair Darling
      </a>
      had given permission for up to £150 billion to be created if necessary.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Times-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was expected that the process would occur over a period of three months with results only likely in the long term.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_65-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BBC-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 5 November 2009, some £175 billion had been injected using quantitative easing and the effectiveness of the process remained less successful in the long term. In July 2012, the final increase in the asset purchases finance meant QE had peaked at £375 billion, then holding solely UK Government bonds, representing one third of the UK national debt.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BofEAPF_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BofEAPF-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The result of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">
       2016 UK referendum on EU membership
      </a>
      caused a major decline in the pound against other world currencies as the future of international trade relationships and domestic political leadership became unclear.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The referendum result weakened sterling against euro overnight by 5%. The night before the vote £1 was trading for €1.30; on the day following the referendum, when the result was clear, £1 was trading at €1.23. By October 2016, the exchange rate was €1.12 to the pound, a fall of 14% since the referendum.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Against the US dollar, meanwhile, the pound fell from $1.466 to $1.3694 when the referendum result was first revealed, and down to $1.2232 by October 2016, a fall of 16%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_inflation_rate">
       Annual inflation rate
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Annual inflation rate">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Bank of England had stated (2009) that the decision had been taken to prevent the rate of
      <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
       inflation
      </a>
      falling below the 2% target rate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNN1_66-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CNN1-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mervyn King, the Governor of the Bank of England, also had suggested there were no other monetary options left as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interest_rates" title="Interest rates">
       interest rates
      </a>
      had already been cut to their lowest level ever (0.5%) and it was unlikely that they would be cut further.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Times-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The inflation rate per annum rose in following years, reaching 5.2% (based on Consumer Price Index) in September 2011, then decreased to around 2.5% in the following year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Coins">
       Coins
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Coins">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">
       Coins of the pound sterling
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-decimal_coins">
       Pre-decimal coins
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Pre-decimal coins">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The silver penny (plural:
      <i>
       pence
      </i>
      ; abbreviation:
      <i>
       d
      </i>
      ) was the principal and often the only coin in circulation from the 8th century until the 13th century. Although some fractions of the penny were struck (see
      <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">
       farthing
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British coin)">
       halfpenny
      </a>
      ), it was more common to find pennies cut into halves and quarters to provide smaller change. Very few gold coins were struck, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Gold_penny" title="Gold penny">
       gold penny
      </a>
      (worth 20 silver pence) a rare example. However, in 1279, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Groat_(coin)" title="Groat (coin)">
        groat
       </a>
      </i>
      , worth 4d, was introduced, with the half groat following in 1344. 1344 also saw the establishment of a gold coinage with the introduction (after the failed
      <a href="/wiki/Florin_(English_coin)" title="Florin (English coin)">
       gold florin
      </a>
      ) of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Noble_(English_coin)" title="Noble (English coin)">
        noble
       </a>
      </i>
      worth six shillings and eight pence (6/8) (i.e. 3 nobles to the pound), together with the half and quarter noble. Reforms in 1464 saw a reduction in value of the coinage in both silver and gold, with the noble renamed the
      <i>
       ryal
      </i>
      and worth 10/- (i.e. 2 to the pound) and the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Angel_(coin)" title="Angel (coin)">
        angel
       </a>
      </i>
      introduced at the noble's old value of 6/8.
     </p>
     <p>
      The reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">
       Henry VII
      </a>
      saw the introduction of two important coins: the
      <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">
       shilling
      </a>
      (abbr.:
      <i>
       s
      </i>
      ; known as the
      <i>
       testoon
      </i>
      ) in 1487 and the pound (known as the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">
        sovereign
       </a>
      </i>
      , abbr.:
      <i>
       £
      </i>
      or
      <i>
       L
      </i>
      ) in 1489. In 1526, several new denominations of gold coins were added, including the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_(British_coin)" title="Crown (British coin)">
        crown
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Half_crown_(British_coin)" title="Half crown (British coin)">
        half crown
       </a>
      </i>
      worth five shillings (
      <i>
       5/-
      </i>
      ), and two shillings and six pence (
      <i>
       2/6
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       two and six
      </i>
      ) respectively.
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">
       Henry VIII
      </a>
      's reign (1509–1547) saw a high level of
      <a href="/wiki/Debasement" title="Debasement">
       debasement
      </a>
      which continued into the reign of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI" title="Edward VI">
       Edward VI
      </a>
      (1547–1553). This debasement was halted in 1552, and a new silver coinage was introduced, including coins for 1d, 2d,
      <a href="/wiki/Threepence_(British_coin)" title="Threepence (British coin)">
       3d
      </a>
      , 4d and
      <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">
       6d
      </a>
      , 1/-, 2/6 and 5/-. In the reign of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I" title="Elizabeth I">
       Elizabeth I
      </a>
      (1558–1603), silver
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
      </span>
      d and
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       1
       <span class="visualhide">
       </span>
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
      d coins were added, but these denominations did not last. Gold coins included the half-crown, crown, angel, half-sovereign and sovereign. Elizabeth's reign also saw the introduction of the horse-drawn screw press to produce the first "milled" coins.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the succession of the Scottish King
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_VI" title="James VI">
       James VI
      </a>
      to the English throne, a new gold coinage was introduced, including the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spur_ryal" title="Spur ryal">
        spur ryal
       </a>
      </i>
      (15/-), the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Unite_(English_coin)" title="Unite (English coin)">
        unite
       </a>
      </i>
      (20/-) and the
      <i>
       rose ryal
      </i>
      (30/-). The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Laurel_(English_coin)" title="Laurel (English coin)">
        laurel
       </a>
      </i>
      , worth 20/-, followed in 1619. The first base metal coins were also introduced:
      <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">
       tin
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">
       copper
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Farthing_(British_coin)" title="Farthing (British coin)">
       farthings
      </a>
      . Copper
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halfpenny_(British_coin)" title="Halfpenny (British coin)">
       halfpenny
      </a>
      coins followed in the reign of
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">
       Charles I
      </a>
      . During the
      <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
       English Civil War
      </a>
      , a number of siege coinages were produced, often in unusual denominations.
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the restoration of the monarchy in 1660, the coinage was reformed, with the ending of production of hammered coins in 1662. The
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" title="Guinea (British coin)">
        guinea
       </a>
      </i>
      was introduced in 1663, soon followed by the
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
      , 2 and 5 guinea coins. The silver coinage consisted of denominations of 1d, 2d, 3d, 4d and 6d, 1/-, 2/6 and 5/-. Due to the widespread export of silver in the 18th century, the production of silver coins gradually came to a halt, with the half crown and crown not issued after the 1750s, the 6d and 1/- stopping production in the 1780s. In response, copper 1d and 2d coins and a gold
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
      </span>
      guinea (7/-) were introduced in 1797. The copper penny was the only one of these coins to survive long.
     </p>
     <p>
      To alleviate the shortage of silver coins, between 1797 and 1804, the Bank of England counterstamped
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dollar" title="Spanish dollar">
       Spanish dollars
      </a>
      (8 reales) and other
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_real" title="Spanish real">
       Spanish
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonial_real" title="Spanish colonial real">
       Spanish colonial
      </a>
      coins for circulation. A small counterstamp of the King's head was used. Until 1800, these circulated at a rate of 4/9 for 8 reales. After 1800, a rate of 5/- for 8 reales was used. The Bank then issued silver tokens for 5/- (struck over Spanish dollars) in 1804, followed by tokens for 1/6 and 3/- between 1811 and 1816.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1816, a new silver coinage was introduced in denominations of 6d, 1/-, 2/6 (half-crown) and 5/- (crown). The crown was only issued intermittently until 1900. It was followed by a new gold coinage in 1817 consisting of 10/- and £1 coins, known as the half sovereign and
      <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_(British_coin)" title="Sovereign (British coin)">
       sovereign
      </a>
      . The silver 4d coin was reintroduced in 1836, followed by the 3d in 1838, with the 4d coin issued only for colonial use after 1855. In 1848, the 2/-
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florin_(British_coin)" title="Florin (British coin)">
        florin
       </a>
      </i>
      was introduced, followed by the short-lived
      <a href="/wiki/Double_florin" title="Double florin">
       double florin
      </a>
      in 1887. In 1860, copper was replaced by bronze in the farthing (quarter penny,
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        4
       </sub>
      </span>
      d), halfpenny and penny.
     </p>
     <p>
      During the
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       First World War
      </a>
      , production of the half sovereign and sovereign was suspended, and although the gold standard was later restored, the coins saw little circulation thereafter. In 1920, the silver standard, maintained at .925 since 1552, was reduced to .500. In 1937, a nickel-brass 3d coin was introduced; the last silver 3d coins were issued seven years later. In 1947, the remaining silver coins were replaced with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cupro-nickel" title="Cupro-nickel">
       cupro-nickel
      </a>
      , with the exception of
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Maundy#Maundy_coinage" title="Royal Maundy">
       Maundy coinage
      </a>
      which was then restored to .925. Inflation caused the farthing to cease production in 1956 and be demonetised in 1960. In the run-up to decimalisation, the halfpenny and half-crown were demonetised in 1969.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Decimal_coins">
       Decimal coins
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Decimal coins">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table align="right" style="border: 1px #cccccc solid; border-collapse: collapse; background: #f9f9f9; margin: 0 0 0 1em; clear:right;">
      <tr>
       <th colspan="2" style="width:216px;padding:2px;">
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/One_pound_(British_decimal_coin)" title="One pound (British decimal coin)">
         £1
        </a>
        coin (Welsh design, 2000)
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td colspan="2" style="padding:0px; border: 1px #cccccc solid;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1pound2000front.jpg">
         <img alt="1pound2000front.jpg" data-file-height="239" data-file-width="240" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/1pound2000front.jpg/110px-1pound2000front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/1pound2000front.jpg/165px-1pound2000front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/1pound2000front.jpg/220px-1pound2000front.jpg 2x" width="110"/>
        </a>
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uk1pnd2000.jpg">
         <img alt="Uk1pnd2000.jpg" data-file-height="244" data-file-width="240" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/Uk1pnd2000.jpg/110px-Uk1pnd2000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/Uk1pnd2000.jpg/165px-Uk1pnd2000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fc/Uk1pnd2000.jpg/220px-Uk1pnd2000.jpg 2x" width="110"/>
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr style="font-size: 85%; vertical-align: top;">
       <td style="width:106px;padding:2px; border: 1px #cccccc solid; line-height: 1.5em;">
        <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">
         Elizabeth II
        </a>
       </td>
       <td style="width:106px;padding:2px 1px 2px 2px; border: 1px #cccccc solid; line-height: 1.5em;">
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welsh_dragon" title="Welsh dragon">
         Welsh dragon
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      British coinage timeline:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       1968: The first
       <a href="/wiki/Coins_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Coins of the pound sterling">
        decimal coins
       </a>
       were introduced. These were
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cupro-nickel" title="Cupro-nickel">
        cupro-nickel
       </a>
       5p and 10p coins which were equivalent to, and circulated alongside, the one shilling coin and the two shilling
       <i>
        or florin
       </i>
       coin respectively.
      </li>
      <li>
       1969: The curved equilateral
       <a href="/wiki/Heptagon" title="Heptagon">
        heptagonal
       </a>
       cupro-nickel 50p coin replaced the 10/- note.
      </li>
      <li>
       1971: The decimal coinage was completed when decimalisation came into effect in 1971 with the introduction of the bronze
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       p, 1p and 2p coins and the withdrawal of the 1d and 3d coins.
      </li>
      <li>
       1980: Withdrawal of 6d coins, which had circulated at a value of
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        2
        <span class="visualhide">
        </span>
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       p.
      </li>
      <li>
       1982: The word "new" was dropped from the coinage and a 20p coin was introduced.
      </li>
      <li>
       1983: A £1 coin was introduced.
      </li>
      <li>
       1983: The
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       p coin was last produced.
      </li>
      <li>
       1984: The
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       p coin was
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/demonetise" title="wikt:demonetise">
        demonetised
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       1990: The crown, worth 25p, was re-tariffed for future issues as a commemorative coin at £5.
      </li>
      <li>
       1990s: The 5p, 10p and 50p coins became smaller.
      </li>
      <li>
       1991: Pre-decimal 1/- coins, which had continued to circulate with a value of 5p, were demonetised in 1991 after the 5p coin became smaller. At the same time larger first generation decimal 5p coins were demonetised.
      </li>
      <li>
       1992:
       <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">
        Bronze
       </a>
       was replaced with
       <a href="/wiki/Copper_plating" title="Copper plating">
        copper-plated
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">
        steel
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       1993: Pre-decimal 2/- coins,
       <i>
        or florin,
       </i>
       a legacy of the 1848 attempt at decimalisation were demonetised. At the same time larger first generation decimal 10p coins were demonetised.
      </li>
      <li>
       1998: The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bi-metallic_coins" title="Bi-metallic coins">
        bi-metallic
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_pounds_(British_decimal_coin)#The_modern_circulating_coin_.281997.E2.80.93present.29" title="Two pounds (British decimal coin)">
        £2 coin
       </a>
       was introduced.
      </li>
      <li>
       2007: By now the value of copper in the pre-1992 1p and 2p coins (which are 97% copper) exceeded those coins' face value to such an extent that melting down the coins by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" title="Entrepreneur">
        entrepreneurs
       </a>
       was becoming worthwhile (with a premium of up to 11%, with smelting costs reducing this to around 4%)—although this is illegal, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Market_value" title="Market value">
        market value
       </a>
       of copper has subsequently fallen dramatically from these earlier peaks.
      </li>
      <li>
       2012: The 5p and 10p coins were changed from cupro-nickel to
       <a href="/wiki/Nickel_electroplating" title="Nickel electroplating">
        nickel-plated steel
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       2014: It was announced that in 2017 the pound coin will change to a more secure twelve-sided coin to discourage forgery.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      At present, the oldest circulating coins in the UK are the 1p and 2p copper coins introduced in 1971. No other coins from before 1980 are in circulation. Before decimalisation, a handful of change might have contained coins 100 or more years old, bearing any of five monarchs' heads.
     </p>
     <p>
      In April 2008, an extensive redesign of the coinage was unveiled. The new designs were issued gradually into circulation, starting in mid-2008. The new reverses of the 1p, 2p, 5p, 10p, 20p and 50p coins feature parts of the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_coat_of_arms_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal coat of arms of the United Kingdom">
       Royal Shield
      </a>
      , and the new pound coin depicts the whole shield. The coins have the same sizes, shapes and weights as those with the old designs, which will continue to circulate.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Banknotes">
       Banknotes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Banknotes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_pound_sterling" title="Banknotes of the pound sterling">
       Banknotes of the pound sterling
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bank_Of_England10.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="113" data-file-width="398" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Bank_Of_England10.png/300px-Bank_Of_England10.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/71/Bank_Of_England10.png 1.5x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bank_Of_England10.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        £10 Series E (revised) Bank of England note.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first sterling notes were issued by the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      shortly after its foundation in 1694. Denominations were initially written on the notes at the time of issue. From 1745, the notes were printed in denominations between £20 and £1000, with any odd shillings added by hand. £10 notes were added in 1759, followed by £5 in 1793 and £1 and £2 in 1797. The lowest two denominations were withdrawn following the end of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">
       Napoleonic wars
      </a>
      . In 1855, the notes were converted to being entirely printed, with denominations of £5, £10, £20, £50, £100, £200, £300, £500 and £1000 issued.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_Scotland_20_pound_note_2007.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="418" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d1/Bank_of_Scotland_20_pound_note_2007.jpg/300px-Bank_of_Scotland_20_pound_note_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d1/Bank_of_Scotland_20_pound_note_2007.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_Scotland_20_pound_note_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A £20 note of the 2007 issue from the
        <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">
         Bank of Scotland
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">
       Bank of Scotland
      </a>
      began issuing notes in 1695. Although the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound_scots" title="Pound scots">
       pound scots
      </a>
      was still the currency of Scotland, these notes were denominated in sterling in values up to £100. From 1727, the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">
       Royal Bank of Scotland
      </a>
      also issued notes. Both banks issued some notes denominated in guineas as well as pounds. In the 19th century, regulations limited the smallest note issued by Scottish banks to be the £1 denomination, a note not permitted in England.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_Ireland_5_pound_note_2008.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="110" data-file-width="418" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Bank_of_Ireland_5_pound_note_2008.jpg/300px-Bank_of_Ireland_5_pound_note_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Bank_of_Ireland_5_pound_note_2008.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_Ireland_5_pound_note_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A £5 sterling note of the 2008 issue from the
        <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Ireland" title="Bank of Ireland">
         Bank of Ireland
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      With the extension of sterling to Ireland in 1825, the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Ireland" title="Bank of Ireland">
       Bank of Ireland
      </a>
      began issuing sterling notes, later followed by other Irish banks. These notes included the unusual denominations of 30/- and £3. The highest denomination issued by the Irish banks was £100.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1826, banks at least 65 miles (105 km) from London were given permission to issue their own paper money. From 1844, new banks were excluded from issuing notes in England and Wales but not in Scotland and Ireland. Consequently, the number of private banknotes dwindled in England and Wales but proliferated in Scotland and Ireland. The last English private banknotes were issued in 1921.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1914, the
      <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">
       Treasury
      </a>
      introduced notes for 10/- and £1 to replace gold coins. These circulated until 1928 when they were replaced by Bank of England notes. Irish independence reduced the number of Irish banks issuing sterling notes to five operating in
      <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">
       Northern Ireland
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       Second World War
      </a>
      had a drastic effect on the note production of the Bank of England. Fearful of mass forgery by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi" title="Nazi">
       Nazis
      </a>
      (see
      <a href="/wiki/Operation_Bernhard" title="Operation Bernhard">
       Operation Bernhard
      </a>
      ), all notes for £10 and above ceased production, leaving the bank to issue only 10/-, £1 and £5 notes. Scottish and Northern Irish issues were unaffected, with issues in denominations of £1, £5, £10, £20, £50 and £100.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Bank of England reintroduced £10 notes in 1964. In 1969, the 10/- note was replaced by the 50p coin as part of the preparation for decimalisation. £20 Bank of England notes were reintroduced in 1970, followed by £50 in 1981.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the introduction of the £1 coin in 1983, Bank of England £1 notes were withdrawn in 1988. Scottish and Northern Irish banks followed, with only the Royal Bank of Scotland continuing to issue this denomination.
     </p>
     <p>
      UK notes include raised print (e.g. on the words "Bank of England"); watermarks; embedded metallic thread; holograms; and fluorescent ink visible only under
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_lamp" title="UV lamp">
       UV lamps
      </a>
      . Three printing techniques are involved: offset litho, intaglio and letterpress; and incorporate a total of 85 specialized inks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The £5
      <a href="/wiki/Polymer_banknote" title="Polymer banknote">
       polymer banknote
      </a>
      , issued by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Bank" title="Northern Bank">
       Northern Bank
      </a>
      (now
      <a href="/wiki/Danske_Bank_(Northern_Ireland)" title="Danske Bank (Northern Ireland)">
       Danske Bank
      </a>
      ) in 2000, was the only polymer note in circulation until 2016, although Danske Bank also produces paper-based £10, £20 and £50 notes. The Bank of England introduced £5 polymer banknotes in September 2016.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Bank of England produces notes named "giant" and "titan".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A giant is a one million pound note, and a titan is a one hundred million pound bank note,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      of which there are about 40. Giants and titans are used only within the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banking_system" title="Banking system">
       banking system
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Monetary_policy">
       Monetary policy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Monetary policy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      As the
      <a href="/wiki/Central_bank" title="Central bank">
       central bank
      </a>
      of the United Kingdom which has been delegated authority by the government, the
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      sets the
      <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">
       monetary policy
      </a>
      for the British pound by controlling the amount of money in circulation. It has a monopoly on issuance of banknotes in England and Wales, and regulates the amount of banknotes issued by seven authorized banks in Scotland and Northern Ireland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">
       HM Treasury
      </a>
      has reserve powers to give orders to the committee "if they are required in the public interest and by extreme economic circumstances" but such orders must be endorsed by Parliament within 28 days.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Unlike banknotes which have separate issuers in Scotland and Northern Ireland, all UK coins are issued by the
      <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">
       Royal Mint
      </a>
      , which is an independent enterprise (wholly owned by the Treasury) which also mints coins for other countries.
     </p>
     <p>
      In Britain's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_Dependencies" title="Crown Dependencies">
       Crown Dependencies
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Manx_pound" title="Manx pound">
       Manx pound
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jersey_pound" title="Jersey pound">
       Jersey pound
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Guernsey_pound" title="Guernsey pound">
       Guernsey pound
      </a>
      are unregulated by the Bank of England and are issued independently.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, they are maintained at a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_exchange_rate" title="Fixed exchange rate">
       fixed exchange rate
      </a>
      by their respective governments, and Bank of England notes have been made legal tender on the islands, forming a sort of one-way de facto
      <a href="/wiki/Currency_union" title="Currency union">
       currency union
      </a>
      . These currencies do not have
      <a href="/wiki/ISO_4217" title="ISO 4217">
       ISO 4217
      </a>
      codes so "GBP" is usually used to represent all of them; informal codes are used where the difference is important.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">
       British Overseas Territories
      </a>
      are responsible for the monetary policy of their own currencies (where they exist),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and have their own ISO 4217 codes. The
      <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands_pound" title="Falkland Islands pound">
       Falkland Islands pound
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar_pound" title="Gibraltar pound">
       Gibraltar pound
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Helena_pound" title="Saint Helena pound">
       Saint Helena pound
      </a>
      are set at a fixed 1:1 exchange rate with the British pound by local governments.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_tender_and_national_issues">
       Legal tender and national issues
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legal tender and national issues">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sterling_area.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1112" data-file-width="753" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sterling_area.svg/220px-Sterling_area.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sterling_area.svg/330px-Sterling_area.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sterling_area.svg/440px-Sterling_area.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sterling_area.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/British_Islands" title="British Islands">
         British Islands
        </a>
        (red) and overseas territories (blue) using the Pound or their local issue.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Legal_tender" title="Legal tender">
       Legal tender
      </a>
      in the United Kingdom is defined such that "a debtor cannot successfully be sued for non-payment if he pays into court in legal tender." Parties can alternatively settle a debt by other means with mutual consent. Strictly speaking it is necessary for the debtor to offer the exact amount due as there is no obligation for the other party to provide
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/change" title="wiktionary:change">
       change
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mint_LT_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mint_LT-81">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Throughout the UK, £1 and £2 coins are legal tender for any amount, with the other coins being legal tender only for limited amounts.
      <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">
       Bank of England
      </a>
      notes are legal tender for any amount in
      <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">
       England and Wales
      </a>
      , but not in Scotland or
      <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">
       Northern Ireland
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      (Bank of England 10/- and £1 notes were legal tender, as were Scottish banknotes, during
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      under the Currency (Defence) Act 1939, repealed on 1 January 1946.)
      <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">
       Channel Islands
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
       Isle of Man
      </a>
      banknotes are legal tender only in their respective jurisdictions.
     </p>
     <p>
      Bank of England, Scottish, Northern Irish, Channel Islands, Isle of Man, Gibraltar, and Falkland banknotes may be offered anywhere in the UK, although there is no obligation to accept them as a means of payment, and acceptance varies. For example, merchants in England generally accept Scottish and Northern Irish bills, but some unfamiliar with them may reject them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Merchants in England generally do not accept Jersey, Guernsey, Isle of Man, Gibraltar, and Falkland notes, but Isle of Man notes are generally accepted in Scotland and Northern Ireland. Bank of England notes are generally accepted in the Falklands and Gibraltar, but for example Scottish and Northern Irish notes are not.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since all of the bills are denominated in pounds sterling, banks will exchange them for locally issued bills at face value, though some in the UK have had trouble exchanging Falkland Islands pounds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falklands_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Falklands-85">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Commemorative
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_pounds_(British_decimal_coin)" title="Five pounds (British decimal coin)">
       £5
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/British_twenty-five_pence_coin" title="British twenty-five pence coin">
       25p
      </a>
      (crown) coins, rarely seen in circulation, are legal tender, as are the bullion coins issued by the Mint.
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Value">
       Value
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Value">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 2006, the
      <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_Library" title="House of Commons Library">
       House of Commons Library
      </a>
      published a research paper which included an index of prices in pounds for each year between 1750 and 2005, where 1974 was indexed at 100.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [88]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Regarding the period 1750–1914 the document states: "Although there was considerable year on year fluctuation in price levels prior to 1914 (reflecting the quality of the harvest, wars, etc.) there was not the long-term steady increase in prices associated with the period since 1945". It goes on to say that "Since 1945 prices have risen in every year with an aggregate rise of over 27 times".
     </p>
     <p>
      The value of the index in 1751 was 5.1, increasing to a peak of 16.3 in 1813 before declining very soon after the end of the
      <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">
       Napoleonic Wars
      </a>
      to around 10.0 and remaining in the range 8.5–10.0 at the end of the nineteenth century. The index was 9.8 in 1914 and peaked at 25.3 in 1920, before declining to 15.8 in 1933 and 1934—prices were only about three times as high as they had been 180 years earlier.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [89]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
       Inflation
      </a>
      had a dramatic effect during and after
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      —the index was 20.2 in 1940, 33.0 in 1950, 49.1 in 1960, 73.1 in 1970, 263.7 in 1980, 497.5 in 1990, 671.8 in 2000 and 757.3 in 2005.
     </p>
     <p>
      The following table shows the equivalent amount of goods and services that, in a particular year, could be purchased with £1.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        [90]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        [91]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The table shows that from 1971 through 2015 the British pound has lost about 92% of its buying power.
     </p>

     <p>
      The smallest coin in 1971 was the
      <span class="nowrap">
      </span>
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       ⁄
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
      p, worth about 6.4p in 2015 prices.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Exchange_rate">
       Exchange rate
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Exchange rate">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The pound is freely bought and sold on the
      <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">
       foreign exchange markets
      </a>
      around the world, and its value relative to other currencies therefore fluctuates.
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 7 October 2016
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , £1 was worth
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">
       US$
      </a>
      1.24,
      <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">
       €
      </a>
      1.11,
      <a href="/wiki/Japanese_yen" title="Japanese yen">
       ¥
      </a>
      128,
      <a href="/wiki/Swiss_franc" title="Swiss franc">
       CHF
      </a>
      1.22,
      <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">
       A$
      </a>
      1.64,
      <a href="/wiki/Canadian_dollar" title="Canadian dollar">
       C$
      </a>
      1.65 or
      <a href="/wiki/Indian_rupee" title="Indian rupee">
       INR
      </a>
      82.83.
     </p>
     <table class="toccolours" style="margin: 0.5em auto; text-align: center;">

        <a class="external text" href="http://fxtop.com/en/currency-pair.php?C1=JPY&amp;C2=GBP" rel="nofollow">
         JPY
        </a>
        <a class="external text" href="http://fxtop.com/en/currency-pair.php?C1=USD&amp;C2=GBP" rel="nofollow">
         USD
        </a>
        <a class="external text" href="http://fxtop.com/en/currency-pair.php?C1=INR&amp;C2=GBP" rel="nofollow">
         INR
        </a>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reserve">
       Reserve
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pound_sterling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Reserve">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Sterling is used as a
      <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">
       reserve currency
      </a>
      around the world and is currently ranked 3rd in value held as reserves. The percental composition of currencies of official foreign
      <a href="/wiki/Reserve_currency" title="Reserve currency">
       exchange reserves
      </a>
      since 1995.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        [92]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        [93]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        [94]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <br/>
     </p>
     <div class="timeline-wrapper">
      <map name="timeline_793034267f35589565352f3c80a65969">
      </map>
      <img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/793034267f35589565352f3c80a65969.png" usemap="#timeline_793034267f35589565352f3c80a65969"/>
     </div>
